Let's keep riffing.\"  \nBecause thatâ€™s what most people do in the wild: they go with it.\n\n* * *\n\nğŸ¤” Why This Becomes a Problem\n-----------------------------\n\n### 1\\. **No Grounding â†’ No Tether to Reality**\n\nBecause LLMs are trained on _text_, not _truth_, they don't have any concept of reality being something separate from whatâ€™s common or coherent in their training corpus.\n\nThey learn **plausibility**, not **verifiability**.\n\nSo when you ask:\n\n> â€œCan you show me the Linux command to enable quantum threads?â€\n\nIt might invent a fake flag like `--enable-qthr`, because it sounds real.\n\n### 2\\. **â€œYes-andâ€ as the Default Behavior**\n\nIt behaves like an improv partner:\n\n> \"You want a unicorn on Mars wearing a NASA helmet? downloading a package and building it from source every time (which can involve compiling C extensions and other nonsense), a **wheel lets you just install the built result** directly.\n\n* * *\n\nğŸ somepackage\n```\n\nThis will:\n\n*   Download the source code for `somepackage`\n*   Build a `.whl` file from it (e.g., `somepackage-1.2.3-py3-none-any.whl`)\n*   Drop that file into your current directory\n\nYou can then install the wheel locally:\n\n```bash\npip install somepackage-1.2.3-py3-none-any.whl\n```\n\nThis is super useful for:\n\n*   **Offline installs** (build once, distribute the `.whl`)\n*   **Caching builds** in environments with slow/limited internet\n*   **Avoiding re-compilation** of source distributions every time\n\n* * *\n\nğŸ§  Summary\n----------\n\n| Term | Meaning |\n| --- | --- |\n| **Wheel (.whl)** | A pre-built Python package â€” like a .deb or .msi file for Python |\n| **`pip wheel`** | A pip command that builds a `.whl` from source for faster install later |\n\n* * *\n\nğŸš§ Side Note: Why this is often confusing\n-----------------------------------------\n\n*   Pythonâ€™s packaging system is notoriously convoluted\n*   Youâ€™ll also hear
